0345404475|5.0|Blade Runner is my favorite film; I first saw it in a Classics class as the story of Roy Batty paralleled legend of Achilles. I actually heard a lot of negative things about the novel it was loosely based on, \\"Do Androids Dream of Electric Sheep?\\" Many reads told me that the author, Philip K. Dick, was a poor writer with brilliant and trippy ideas. After reading \\"Do Androids Dream of Electric Sheep?,\\" I respectfully disagree. While Philip K. Dick's philosophy and ideas were more compelling than his writing, he was a very talented writer and this novel deserves high praise. Is it radically different than the film? Yes but they both paint fantastical future worlds with different themes and epiphanies for the characters. There are many exciting facets of Blade Runner that are absent in the novel, but the novel features unique intrigue such as the sub-culture of \\"chickenheads\\" and the propaganda of \\"Buster Friendly and His Friendly Friends,\\" which are unique. Blade Runner is future film noir while this novel is more of a meditation during a man's middle life crisis in a wasteland. I think the novel and film should be appreciated on their own terms; one cannot read this novel hoping it will be very similar to the famous adaption.<br /><br />The novel takes place in a futuristic world where the fallout of a nuclear holocaust has made much of the planet uninhabitable and the global population fragmented. The protagonist, Rick Deckard, lives in the San Francisco area, which is less affected by radiation than most of the world. Mankind has advanced in space travel allowing for off-world colonies that only the wealthy or well-skilled can live luxuriously. The poor, working-class, sickly and unfortunate remain on polluted Earth. Rick Deckard, a reluctant bounty hunter, makes a living hunting down outlawed robotic-humans called androids. Androids are nearly human in every way except their making and lack of human emotions especially empathy. Like many humans still on Earth, he is obsessed with superficial consumerism, particularly the status symbol of animals since the nearly extinct creatures are so incredibly expensive. Many middle class people buy electric versions of animals as a cover for appearing to have an actual, much-coveted pet. As his distant wife becomes hooked on electronic, emotional transmitters called empathy boxes, Deckard faces an existential crisis as he hunts down androids including a rebelling new group as well as searching for inner peace.<br /><br />As Deckard is assigned the task of hunting various androids who are illegally on Earth and are either in hiding secretively or hiding in plain sight by assuming human identities. Androids are treated as unwanted contraband on Earth and as slaves on the off-world colonies. The lack of feelings by androids leaves them to coldly cruel acts at time, such as when they pluck the legs off a spider just to see what will happen to it. One of Deckard's tasks involves testing a suspected android through interrogation using a physiological detector called the Voigt-Kampff machine. This turns out to be a trap by a corporative competitor who wishes to fool people into believing that the Voigt-Kampff produces faulty results and is not reliable.<br /><br />Deckard is further sidetracked at one point when he is brought into a bizarre, almost unreal police station where he is interrogated and accused of being an android himself, which could be possible since newer models of androids often had false memories installed unbeknownst into them. He later surmises that this surreal office must have been a set-up run by androids. The sometimes sociopathic personalities of his fellow bounty hunters as well as the brutal nature of the job leads Rick Deckard to ponder what difference is there between his current self and the empathy-less androids he hunts.<br /><br />Deckard continues to obsess materialistically over finding a deal for buying livestock, even if he could not afford it. Deckard findsoccasional inspiration in his esoteric New Age religion, Mercerism, which guides him to the climax of his philosophical and spiritual journey where he speaks to what appears to be the prophet Wilbur Mercer. Mercer's specter warns Deckard of the hypocrisy of people, the world and Mercerism as well as letting him know that Mercer is not human. When Deckard realizes that one of the escaped androids whose partners he killed sought revenge by killing his pet rather than dazed wife; he realizes the cheapness of material possessions. The novel's most prevalent theme is accepting somber knowledge over ignorant bliss; the ability to appreciate the reality of the situation even if you wish it were not so. This is demonstrated at the very end when Deckard spots a toad, Mercer's patron (and now-extinct) animal, but finds acceptance in knowing that it is electric.<br /><br />Philip K. Dick's novel provides insight on what people can act like without empathy and our relationship with reality while painting a fantastic portrait of a bizarre, dangerous world of the future. The sub-culture of chickenheads in a desperate wasteland is interesting but the fictional religion of Mercerism, complete with a self-admitted false prophet, is fantastic in its fascinating possibilities. The emphasis on empathy and shared experiences of suffering blends and ties together the morality of caring and the immorality of sadism. Deckard's epiphany is paired with a brilliant and wise message from Mercer, one that highlights the insight Philip K. Dick had on the human condition, from the fact that salvation does not exist:<br /><br />\\"You will be required to do wrong no matter where you go. It is the basic condition of life, to be required to violate your own identity. At some time, every creature which lives must do so. It is the ultimate shadow, the defeat of creation; this is the curse at work, the curse that feeds on all life. Everywhere in the universe.\\"<br /><br />Is this true of our existence? Read the novel and think about it.occasional inspiration in his esoteric New Age religion, Mercerism, which guides him to the climax of his philosophical and spiritual journey where he speaks to what appears to be the prophet Wilbur Mercer. It's about the end of the world, and starts on the anniversary of the end of the world for the Japanese, the day the atom bomb was dropped on Hiroshima.<br /><br />Our protagonist, Jonah, is writing a book about the end of the world.  He ends up interviewing the offspring of the father of the atom bomb, Dr. Felix Hoenikker, an eccentric, seemingly uncaring father who, unbeknownst to most, also makes \\"Ice Nine\\" before he dies.  Ice Nine causes everything to freeze.  It precipates the end of the world.<br /><br />Before Ice Nine takes over, and freezes the world, Vonnegut takes us on a whirlwind tour of the hearts and minds of a slew of zany characters, from Newt the midget, son of The Father of The Atom Bomb, to a secretary who disdains anyone who \\"thinks too much,\\" to a philanthropist who turns out to be the complete opposite, to a zillion others (almost too many) in between.  We are taken to the island of San Lorenzo, a Carribean banana republic run by a paranoind, eccentric dictator that is totally at the beck and call of the USA, and that is where things really get weird.<br /><br />This book is divided into 127 very short chapters, which makes it not only easier to read and remember, but also made it easier, I'm sure, for Mr. Vonnegut to write.<br /><br />One of the main points of this story, if I understand correctly, is that religion, in this case \\"Bokonism,\\" is pretty much a hodge-podge/hocus-pocus bed of spectacular lies.  In the end, the founder of Bokonism, Bokonen, admits himself that it was all a complete joke, not to be taken sersiously (how can anyone take seriously a religion where people make love by rubbing their feet together?), but I won't spoil it by telling you what happens in between.<br /><br />Mad scientists and their nervous secretaries, midget kung-fu, foot sex, countless hungry, skinny, stupid natives, one beautiful, \\"healthy\\" native, fat businessmen here to save the world, steep jungles, waterfalls, underground bomb shelters, earthquakes, tornadoes, fire and brimstone (don't tell Vonnegut I said that), horse faced flute playing enfent terribles, and much, much more.  Oh, and I almost forgot.  Ice Nine.  How could I forget Ice Nine?<br /><br />This book is a parable for the end of time.to save the world, steep jungles, waterfalls, underground bomb shelters, earthquakes, tornadoes, fire and brimstone (don't tell Vonnegut I said that), horse faced flute playing enfent terribles, and much, much more. The concept--using your smart phone/tablet to view your baby from anywhere you can get wifi--is spot-on and likely to be a big money maker for someone but this is not the product that will corner this market. The camera itself seems to be high quality and well built but it's all the software quirks that make this product pretty much useless. For starters, the App for iOS (iPad, iPhone, iPod) that is required to use, view, control and setup the camera is clunky and far from user-friendly. I am in the marketing field and have seen my fair share of ugly/unusable apps but this one takes the cake. It almost feels unfinished--but in a state that should never have been released to the public, certainly no \\"working beta\\" version since it barely works.<br /><br />Here are the issues with the app:<br />1. User interface is unappealing and clunky (like it was created in a very foreign language and *poorly* translated)<br />2. Difficult to locate settings and features--what should be straightforward is hidden in sub-menus, etc.<br />3. The software lags a LOT. And by lag I mean the time it takes for you to see/hear on your device what is caught on camera as well as the time it takes for your movement command to control the camera. On average we experienced a lag of 30+ secs but at times as much as 1 minute. Example: I set the alert feature to alert us when there was movement or sound in the room but even after jumping up and down, clapping and screaming in the room, the alert didn't hit my phone for at least a minute. In fact I had given up on it working and was in another room when it finally went off. This is a huge deal to me, I mean what is the good of a monitor if it doesn't monitor in real-time? For the rest of the functions (moving the camera, watching video) the lag was super annoying.<br />4. Hard (if not impossible--I didn't get it to work) to set up camera/wifi/router to allow camera to be viewed and controlled outside the home. Half the reason we got this was to be able to look in on our baby when my wife or I traveled. Setting up your network to allow this is not easy.<br /><br />Issues with the camera:<br />1. B0014YVC6W|2.0|Almost all motion pictures are made to please the audience. A film needn't have a happy ending to be pleasing; people are looking for some form of emotional connection, and this can include (for example) a hero who dies in the process of achieving something worthwhile.<br /><br />But it's rare to see a film that so obviously panders to the audience's desire for the superficially appealing as does \\"Short Circuit\\". It's coldly calculated to push all the right buttons.<br /><br />In terms of acting, directing, & production values, \\"Short Circuit\\" is in no way a bad film. The two-star rating is for the way it so blatantly sucks up to the audience's desire for cheaply obtained emotional reactions.<br /><br />\\"Short Circuit\\" is strictly a kiddie film, with little of any real interest to adults.<br /><br />The running commentary (by the director and writers) is one of those rarities -- a generally interesting narrative that largely avoids the self-congratulatory smugness and pointless anecdotes that mar too many commentaries. 0756405890|5.0|Over the years, I've become less and less interested in the \\"high fantasy\\" genre, as the novels I would pick up all seemed formulaic, bloated, predictable, boring, and about 500 pages longer than they needed to be. After hearing so many good things said about \\"The Name of the Wind\\", I finally decided to give it a shot, and it was definitely a good investment of time. This novel is gripping, very well written, and generally resists the tired tropes of the genre.<br /><br />The one thing that sets this book apart is the depth of character for the protagonist, Kvothe (spelling? ...I listened to this book on audio). He is such a fascinating individual, and his mastery of such a wide range of skills makes him an extraordinary character. Musician, bard, actor/player, magician/sympathist, swordsman, demon wrangler, and \\"kung fu\\" master (expounded upon in the second novel, \\"The Wise Man's Fear\\"). But rather than just say \\"he was really good at so and so\\", Rothfuss does a marvelous job of explaining how Kvothe acquired each skill at such a particularly high level of mastery. He does this by telling convincing stories from Kvothe's past, which are layered to create his fascinating character. Furthermore, it's clear that Rothfuss did his research. He either knows about these things, or he consulted with those who did. For istance, his details about musicianship are authentic and complex, clearly the result of hard earned knowledge, not invention (I'm sure some creative liberty is taken, but not a whole lot).<br /><br />Just about any reader should enjoy this book, but fantasy fans will love it- even though it's fairly light on the \\"fantasy\\", in favor of character. Listening to it on audio was particularly enjoyable, in my opinion. I felt like I was being told a great story by a bard at the local tavern, as we sat beside the fireplace with mugs of ale in hand ;)

0817439390|5.0|I thought I should review this book here, because I have recommended it many, many times over the years, and beginning with the original edition, have purchased, and given it away to an aspiring photographer at least 3 times over the years.  I have read the few 1 star reviews and have been surprised.  Most of them don't like the book but give no specific, concrete reason why.  Those that do, seem to feel \\"cheated,\\" because it doesn't say \\"beginner's guide\\" and maybe tells them something they already knew.<br /><br />I went back and read the narrative on the Amazon site for each addition.  If you read it carefully, it lays out what the book is going to cover.  It is not intended by the author to be an Ansel Adam's look alike (Ansel already did that as well as can be done, and I'll be the author has read it :-) ).  This book is aimed squarely at someone who is new to a camera that allows you to control the aspects of exposure (for most shooters this means an SLR/DSLR) and wants to know how to do that successfully and correctly.  It is not an in-depth, theory of exposure book.  It is aimed at an audience who needs clear, plain, easy-to-understand explanations that they can take and work with right away.  I have always thought Peterson hit the proverbial home run with this book.  Whenever I have someone start asking me questions about exposure, etc. who is not an experienced photographer, I recommend this book.  I will keep doing so.<br /><br />And, every few years or so, I get out my highlighter and read it again, myself.  Many successful people believe that basis of success is knowing the fundamentals of their craft and practicing them.  Having shot SLR/DSLR cameras for 30 years, I am still surprised to sometimes find (or remember) a gem of wisdom when I go back and read this book.<br /><br />It is not the most sophisticated book on my shelf, but it is a \\"go-to\\" book in many circumstances.  In my view it certainly does what it suggests it will, and what it was intended to do, and does so in a way that has helped many photographers become better at what they do.

B000QCS8TW|2.0|A Game of Thrones is such a joyless tale, all gristle and dry bones.<br /><br />Is it realistic? Not a chance, though it pretends to be. It's purely pessimistic. This is what you would be left with if you sucked all the redeeming qualities out of the world. It's basically, at its heart, a story of disillusionment.<br /><br />Some say he is a good writer, and he is in many ways. His prose is run of the mill, but his dialogue some of the best I've read. It isn't on the nose, and he slips in story in very subtle ways. However, he skips scenes he should show, and only tells about them in flashback, and he pulls away from scenes at the end just to trick the reader, which is just plain bad writing in my opinion. His characters start out as cliches -- the tomboy, the damsel, the spoiled prince, the slow giant who can only say his name, and while some of them pull away from those stereotypes, it takes hundreds and hundreds of pages for them to do it. But the worst thing about the characters is that they are mostly just a bunch of petty and foolish people, no one I would ever want to know, and certainly have no desire to read about. There are a couple interesting characters, and he does give them multiple motivations that sometimes conflict with each other in surprising ways, which is one of the commendable things I can say about him.<br /><br />Confusingly, some have even taken to calling him the American Tolkien. If it were not such an affront to Tolkien it would be quite laughable. What about him is anything like Tolkien? Certainly not the rape and sex scenes, or anything else I can think of. Does he buck tradition? Sure, but not in any way that enriches the tale.<br /><br />If your looking for plot, you should know that this story contains only the inciting incident. Most writers get further into their story the first chapter or two. Maybe the story is epic enough to warrant this, but that really depends how he handles the middle and end of the story, which I definitely won't be reading.<br /><br />If you're a pessimist, enjoy for fill. Otherwise you may find it a meager feast. Notable only for its subtlety, dialogue, and surprising characters.won't be reading.<br /><br />If you're a pessimist, enjoy for fill. 0312641893|4.0|When I first heard about Cinder, I wasn't sure that it was something I would want to read. Cinderella meets cyborgs? It seemed a little out there. But after hearing so many good things about it, I decided to give this book a chance, and I am so glad that I did. Cinder is an incredibly inventive spin on a fairy tale and one that will keep you captivated from beginning to end.<br /><br />Cinder is one of the most well-respected mechanics in New Beijing, but she also has a secret - she's a cyborg. She does everything she can to hide this from others, including always wearing gloves and boots in the market. Her family knows the truth, though, and her stepmother constantly uses Cinder's nature against her. But things begin to change the day that Prince Kai comes to the market. Because not only does Prince Kai come, but also the deadly plague that is sweeping the country strikes those near to Cinder. When her stepmother blames Cinder for her sister's illness, Cinder finds herself handed over to the government to be a research subject for an antidote to the plague. But everyone involved in that research will learn more than they bargained for. And on top of everything else, Cinder's struggles with her family and the plague research, there's still the matter of the prince and the ball...<br /><br />Cinder is nothing like your usual fairy tale retelling. The basic tenets of Cinderella are there - the wicked stepmother, stepsisters, a prince, a ball - but the similarities end there. Cinderella was merely a starting point for Marissa Meyer; she expanded the tale into something new and wonderful all on its own. I really enjoyed Cinder's character, cyborg nature and all. Then again, I really enjoyed all of the characters (wicked stepmother and stepsister aside - those two I wanted to strangle). And Iko, even if she was an android, was such a great best friend. I loved how this story had multiple layers, encompassing not only Cinder's struggle with her family and the plague research but also political crises and the hardships that Prince Kai had to endure. Because Prince Kai...who wouldn't want a prince like that? He loved his people and was so dedicated to doing what was best for them. From family tragedy to political unrest he had to deal with more than any teenager should. And yet, with his kindness, he completely won my heart just like a real prince should.<br /><br />There are many surprising twists and turns along Cinder and Kai's respective journeys, though one important plot twist is fairly obvious early on in the story. Despite that, the many layers and especially the sci-fi elements kept me intrigued and made it nearly impossible to put this book down. The only thing I did wonder about was the setting - it seemed that New Beijing could have just as easily have been any other city if the Chinese names and appellations had been omitted. Knowing the plot, however, I decided I would soldier on until Harold made his decision to walk -- and I urge you, dear reader, to do the same. Because by then I was hooked.<br /><br />There are so many unexpected twists, so many snapshots of small things that are beautiful, that, having just put down the Kindle after reading it all at one go (despite the work waiting), I know I will be back again, to read it in bits, savoring the undertones of sentences, the shapes of paragraphs.<br /><br />I am not certain how to go on with this review without being soppy and sappy, without saying something utterly predictable and boring, such as \\"This book echoes my faith in the basic goodness of humankind.\\"<br /><br />The thing is, though, that it does.<br /><br />Dear reader, choose a day when you don't have deadlines, when you can sit -- preferably in a park -- and give yourself over to the language of the book. The cover was interesting and the synopsis made the story seem unique.  It was about Pi, who is an adult living in Canada reminiscing about his childhood in India to a writer who comes to his house.  Pi tells the writer of his journey from a somewhat well-off life in India to his fight for survival at sea when the Japanese freighter he travels on sinks.  Interestingly enough, Pi ends up sharing the freighter's lifeboat with a Bengal tiger, a zebra, an orangutan, and a hyena, who were animals from his family zoo.<br /><br />When my brother mentioned to me that Life of Pi had been adapted into a film and would be out possibly in November, I realized I had better get started on reading this book before the movie came out!<br /><br />To be honest, I don't know if Life of Pi  is for everyone. And let me tell you, in my opinion, the book moved along faster in the beginning than it did in the later chapters.<br /><br />Despite all this, I did finish the book and I do appreciate the reading, but I don't know if I could ever read it a second time since I found myself skimming through some chapters very quickly. Hah!<br /><br />Another aspect that I enjoyed about this book was the description of Pi's life in India, which included the zoo his  father owned and that he grew up in, his family life, his relationships with others, exploration of life itself (particularly when it came to religion), and his identity at school.  I really liked the short story behind his name, Piscine Molitor Patel and the struggles he went through with it in school.  Those who have unusual, hard to pronounce names, or names that can be easily made fun off can relate to this!  Pi's parents are also interesting characters, particularly his father who definitely has an uncommon and somewhat disturbingly funny way to teach his kids a lesson!  What a way to learn new parenting skills...<br /><br />In the end, Life of Pi left me feeling a little empty because I felt as though I wanted something more to justify the journey I had gone through with the characters; but in a way, the ending made sense because it made the story the narrator told even more compelling.  I guess it's because I'm such a huge Richard Parker fan.

1907444033|5.0|A PRACTICAL APPROACH TO ADVISING ON TAX CREDITS, INCLUDING OVERPAYMENTS<br /><br />An appreciation by Phillip Taylor MBE and Elizabeth Taylor of Richmond Green Chambers<br /><br />If you're a tax adviser, or possibly a family law practitioner dealing with clients plagued by tax problems, you should acquire this book, recently published by Spiramus.<br /><br />Distinguished by its accuracy, completeness and straightforward language, this is an excellent work of reference would also be more than useful to the general reader keen to understand more about how tax credits work... how to claim... and who is entitled to claim in the first place.<br /><br />In case you didn't know or need reminding, LITRG is the Low Incomes Tax Reform Group, described here as `an initiative of the Chartered Institute of Taxation (CIOT) to give a voice to the unrepresented.'<br /><br />Tax credits have not been without controversy and although the UK Coalition government apparently aims to set out a new approach to welfare provision within the next five years, several million families still claim tax credits.<br /><br />All of which mainly explains why numerous tax and welfare advisers have had to acquire an understanding of tax credits in order to advise their clients appropriately.  This book therefore aims to explain clearly, yet in detail, the law and practice of tax credits, for the benefit both of tax specialists and welfare advisers.<br /><br />Within its approximately 350 pages, the book covers the full spectrum of issues pertaining to tax credits, from the history of rules and concepts - relevant ones that is - to the vexed area of overpayments.<br /><br />Speaking of which, if you check out Appendix 4 you can read the full text of the HMRC leaflet on overpayments.  `An overpayment means we've paid you more money than you're entitled to...' it begins cheerily, before going on for another ten or so pages, about the ramifications and procedures.  Small wonder, then, that the powers that be in these matters have been criticized of a number of things, including `poor administration'.<br /><br />All pertinent commentary on tax credits, including working tax credit and child tax credit is included in this useful text. The section on overpayments just mentioned comprises two chapters which explain why overpayments might occur, how to avoid them and how to challenge them.<br /><br />There is considerable emphasis on rights and remedies available to claimants confronted with wrong decisions. 0307341577|5.0|Oh.my.goodness. Every once in a while there is a book that thrills me in every way, shape, and form. Listen here my friends and when you're done, go out and buy this book  Dark Places by Gillian Flynn! I will warn you, the title is exactly what the book is. This is no subtle beach book to be read in the shade on a lovely Sunday with a glass of lemonade. (I know that's my normal routine). This is a search your soul and thank God that you're good and not this kind of evil book. Too dark? No, just the right amount.    First I will start by how I found this gem of a book. If any of you have a Nook and have looked at any of the best-seller lists out there you may see the title \\"Gone Girl\\" keep popping up which is this author's latest. So, I kept seeing it and finally added it to my wish list. Of course once I did that, Barnes & Noble was oh so kind to suggest more titles by this author. This book was her 2nd written and out of the 3 total, had the most interesting plot to me. I will be reading her debut and her newest because this woman has TALENT! She has a gift with words that fellow writers like myself can only dream about. Yes, I can tell a story, and I hope one day someone out there will want to pick up what I'm putting down but Flynn has a mastery for the mystery novel.    The story follows Libby Day, a low-class, low self-esteemed young woman who is really down on her luck in life. When she was a young child, she was the only witness to her mother and sisters' murders and her brother was the one accused of committing the crime. Libby helped put him in jail by testifying against him. Years later, as she runs low on funds, she entertains the ideas of a \\"fan club\\" (of the totally creepy nature) that is convinced her brother is innocent and will pay her to investigate the night all over again and see if she will recant her testimony.    The pace of the book goes back and forth between present time and the day of the murder and is told from different family member's points of view. The anticipation builds as the real story unfolds as to the who's and why's of that terrible, unforgettable night. Libby must decide if she wants to keep searching and go back on everything she had already come to terms with or just let it rest and let her brother rot in jail when he may not be as guilty as he originally seemed.The ending? Amazing! Not at all what I was expecting. I can't say much more without giving it away. The best thing about this book is the way in which Flynn writes. The descriptions, the conversations, the different personalities coming through in the characters. Everyone was so unique and had individual voices. It's not easy to tell a story through different mindsets and this one flows effortlessly. Bravo Gillian! Bravo! I cannot wait to read the next one.    Final Verdict? If you don't read this, you are seriously missing out on a great book. I made my husband read it next and am so anxious to hear his report. B007549YM4|1.0|The opening scenes for The Divide show us a firey nuclear hell unleashed on New York City, a mad dash to get out of a building, and finally we see a lucky few manage to force their way into a basement before the door is slammed shut. The stage has been set for what should be an interesting couple of hours. Maybe a dozen or so characters, seemingly a random bunch that just happened to make it into the same place at the same time.<br /><br />Unfortunately The Divide quickly begins falling apart in many ways. First, within apparently a few days of the total nuclear destruction of New York City an (theoretically) US government agency of some sort has set up shop outside the basement door. This agency is not FEMA out to find survivors and ferry them to safety; no, armed with machine guns, they are out to confront survivors and take by force any children among them, then kill off any adults.<br /><br />Although the sole child in the group is grabbed, injected, bagged, and wisked away from the group, the machine gun armed thugs in futuristic hazmat suits apparently have had almost no training in handling automatic weapons or finding and killing targets. What should have been meat on the table for these thugs turns into a catastrophic loss as they lose two of thier own, including weapons and the suits, for the loss of none of the survivors.<br /><br />The survivors, as well as the audience, wonders what the heck was that all about? So one of them suits up and ventures out. What we find is that this government agency appears to be conducting some kind of disections, tests, or experiments on children. It is near impossible to figure out what they are doing exactly, but what ever it is, it is barbaric, making these government agents seem like some evil aliens from another world. Predictibly, the survivor's disguise doesn't hold up long and he makes a mad dash for the basement, dropping and leaving the machine gun along the way.<br /><br />The government agency apparently decides that welding the door shut from the outside is the best way to keep the rats in the cage, so after this point in the story that door is never opened again. With all of the rats in a well sealed cage and limited supplies with no foreseeable way out, the survivors quickly degenerate into subhuman beings, driven by animal-like instincts and little regard for any kind of community spirit. We are shown torture (both physical and mental), and reapeated rape or attempted rape. Eventually this all leads to a predictable sole survivor who seems to be the one keeping a shred of humanity in an insane environment. This survivor's final act is to shed that last bit of regard for others and purposely abandon the last few to a horrible fate.<br /><br />In the end, the viewer will feel both robbed and cheated. Robbed of two hours of life. Robbed of any explanation for the actions of the mysterious government agency. Cheated by the director's insistance on repeatedly having seeming rational characters make irrational decisions for no plausible reason. Granted, this does happen in real life from time to time, but almost every character in the film repeatedly makes gratuitously bad decisions. It is also hardly believable that a group of somewhat average people would degenerate so quickly into psychotic excuses for humans. If the basement was located in the bottom of Rikers Island prison instead of an average apartment building, this story in this movie might have been a bit more plausible.

Whether the government is \\"inclusive\\" or \\"exclusive\\" in the sharing of power, wealth and opportunity. There is a continuum of governmental \\"styles\\" with the current trend moving toward being less inclusive regarding the opportunity to produce prosperity and more toward being \\"entitlement\\" oriented. I can see why it was so unlikely that anyone would even dream of making a movie.  We are introduced to characters in different geographies and different periods of time, though each story is connected to the others in what appears to be small, insignificant ways, though the impact of what happens in each tale affects the others in ways that aren't immediately apparent.<br />Except for the story at the middle of the book, each tale is split in two, with the first half of the book moving forward in time, until we reach the distant future and a world that seems, in some ways, to share more with the tale that reaches furthest back into the past than all rest.  The second half of the book sends us in reverse, concluding each story while it goes further back in time, tying up both loose ends and tying each story together in so many small yet significant ways.<br />This was both an intriguing and challenging read, and one which stick with me for a long time to come.

B006LSZECO|5.0|This thriller is the literary equivalent of a roller coaster ride. The story unfolds in chapters that offer alternating perspectives of a husband and his wife, who went missing on their fifth anniversary.<br /><br />The husband is the prime suspect and he certainly seems guilty. At first. Then you think he's in the clear. Or not... And is his wife truly a spoiled, overindulged rich girl? Or is she a long-suffering soul trying to reconnect with a distant husband? Or something else entirely? You'll second-guess yourself throughout - it's that kind of book.<br /><br />Flynn pulls you into the story quickly. But every time you think you've figured out what's going on, who's doing what (and to whom), the shift in perspective in the next chapter makes you realize that this is no simple missing persons case.<br /><br />As you are drawn into the book, any allegiances you have for a particular character shift with each chapter. It all seems so basic at first, but as the characters reveal themselves nothing is as simple as it seems.<br /><br />I stayed up late to finish this book - it was that good, and I had to know how it was all going to end. It's one of the most fascinating and unexpected books I've ever read - the ending left me stunned.

B0081HLM2E|4.0|\\"Headhunters\\" (Hodejegeme) is a Norwegian thriller with dark comic overtones, based  on the novel by Jo Neslø. Roger Brown (Aksel Hennie) is a shrewd corporate headhunter. He gets the right person for his company and makes the prospect's reputation with an aggressive attitude and unconventional advice. He's also short and financially overextended. Roger moonlights as an art thief to support a lavish lifestyle and, especially, to impress his beautiful, statuesque wife Diana (Synnøve Macody Lund), whom he always fears will leave him for someone taller and richer. Desperate for cash, bank account overdrawn, Roger needs to score big. He finds his target in a conspicuously charming Dutchman, Clas Greve (Nicolaj Coster-Waldau), who is harboring a Reubens painting in his Oslo apartment. But Greve, a Special Ops veteran and ruthless CEO, will prove to be a more formidable, and more violent, opponent than Roger bargained for.<br /><br />This plot goes over the top for black comedic effect, occasionally bordering on farce. Good writing and Morten Tyldum's deft direction combine with Aksel Hennie's terror and fighting spirit to pull this off. This could just be silly in other hands. But it's funny and suspenseful, with a selfish, insecure, sympathetic protagonist and a handsome, obsessed, almost cartoonish villain. Nicolaj Coster-Waldau is having fun being evil. \\"Headhunters\\" strengths are bizarre twists, one after another, that seem plausible enough but which no one, neither Roger nor the audience, could have predicted and the surprising mettle with which Roger meets these grim challenges. He seems surprised by his own determination and fortitude. The audience certainly is. But Roger keeps going and concocts a few surprises himself. Incidentally, Synnøve Macody Lund, so alluring and natural onscreen, is not a professional actress. She's a Norwegian film critic.<br /><br />The DVD (Magnolia 2012): The film is primarily in Norwegian with optional English, English SDH, and Spanish subtitles. 1933633867|4.0|I found the book deeply engaging at times.  There were some real gems in the parts about ancient history, and Graeber deserves kudos for emphasizing credit as pre-dating money (though he certainly is not the only one).  The author emphasizes cultural/religious aspects of debt and abuses by creditors, but descends to caricature debt as simply designed to suppress the innocent poor.  His grand conclusion: it's not amoral to default on your debts since a debt is \\"perversion of a promise.\\"  Sure there have been abuses by creditors, but I suspect his real beef is not debt but capitalism, war and modernity.  Debt is not the cause but the effect, which we shouldn't have to honor.<br /><br />Curiously, Graeber leaves out a few sacred cows: what about the devastation of socialism (a la Stalin)?  How about suppressing credit standards to meet public policy ends (hint: US homeownership policies since the 90s)?  What about the US government running up debts to unsustainable levels in our name?  Should debt be the only financial obligation we have no moral obligation to pay?  Why should we pay taxes or follow regulations (for which we are obligor/debtors)?  What about the education racket in the US that is reducing our young to debt peons while overpromising post-graduation earnings potential?<br /><br />In addition, he offers up no solutions to better the malevolent world he describes, for example: How should debt be regulated? What is the moral responsibility of the debtor? What are better debt forgiveness mechanisms?  Should there be minimum credit standards?<br /><br />He's the practical anthropologist on matters ancient/tribal and the rambling academic juvenile anarchist on matters recent.  I still give it four stars, for the good parts are really good.

I really love it and it's different, but it is hard to open and I do get weary of wear and tear on the disc after a while, the sleeves they are in aren't that great.<br /><br />The movies themselves, I will review and break down quick. One thing I would like to complain about (and one reason I felt like taking a star off for), the aspect ratio of these movies. Only \\"Aliens\\" (Alien 2 by James Cameron) is filmed in the advertised 2.35:1 ratio. This movie looked the best and filled my entire TV screen. The rest were filmed in the 1.85 ratio... depending on your TV screen size/aspect ratio/setup, maybe the films will look full screen to you, but three out of four films I had to watch with them goddamn annoying black bars on the top and bottom the screen! I hate that when I am already watching on a brand new widescreen TV! Yes, these were the way these films were originally shot, but they should label the technical specs better for these films. Now anyway for the actual movies...<br /><br />ALIEN - 7/10<br />This is the original Alien movie. This was my third time watching the film and first time on Blu Ray. To many's surprise, I'm not that crazy about the original Alien film. In some ways it feels like a rip off of \\"2001 Space Oddessy\\". Still though, the aliens/special effects/camera work are excellent, this is a classic sci fi movie. The story line was good and it introduced us to Alien franchise.<br /><br />ALIENS - 10/10<br />This movie, this was my first time watching it and I have to say this movie alone was worth buying the entire Alien Anthology collection for! This movie rocks. It's got James Cameron written all over it and the always lovable Bill Paxton really compliments this movie. Lovable characters, great camera work/lighting, intense horror scenes, well written script, and I like the aspect ratio it was shot in filling my whole TV. Picture quality, still had that grain but it was just right this time. The sound effects were better mixed in this one and the scary scenes jump out loud when they need to be without being too much. James Cameron knows how to do it right, this movie is a classic and I highly recommend it to anyone whether they are a sci fi fan or not.<br /><br />ALIEN 3 - 6/10<br />This movie was a huge step back from the James Cameron classic. The plot/story sucks forvery obvious reasons although I don't want to put spoilers on here. The camera work, special effects, story, and the rest of it, it just wasn't as good as James Cameron's \\"Aliens\\". Comparing to the original movie it's not as good as that either. This movie was jut bad. I seen it once and I don't want to see it again. Blu ray wise, this is probably among the best of the originals. The picture/sound quality are excellent. No more of that old school grain, everything looks like it's there and you can touch it. In 5.1 surround this movie rocks.<br /><br />ALIEN 4 - 8/10<br />This movie is kind of it's own movie from the original trilogy. It doesn't tie in well with the originals but it's a great sci fi flick and I would watch it again. The camera work, acting, and story are far better than Alien 3 though in my opinion. This movie had the best blu ray quality of all the films. The picture quality is crystal clear, the sound is crystal clear with deep bass tones and rich sound quality well mixed with my 5.1 setup.<br /><br />Overall I welcome these movies into my collection. But I am on the fence for the whole thing.  I try to rate these movies from a \\"general audience\\" stand point. If I am an average person looking to buy these movies for myself or another person as a gift that is not a sci fi fan, are they worth it? To be honest, no. I think the average person should just buy \\"ALIENS\\" (Alien 2), the sequel made my James Cameron. I cannot say enough good things about Aliens. It is a fun, re-watchable movie and ANY audience can enjoy and you can buy it alone on blu ray far cheaper than this entire box set.<br />The rest of these films, the sequels are mediocre. The third film is a train wreck. I feel like never watching it again, and that is a bad feeling to have when you buy a movie that is a permanent addition to your collection of movies.<br /><br />Let me end the review with this. Before you buy, make sure you or the person you are buying these for is an Alien fan. These movies just aren't for everybody. I recommend renting them all and checking them out for yourself. If you think they are fun and you would watch them again over and over, then buy all means buy this box set. Also, if you don't care about that and/or you want the \\"movie theater\\" experience people that doesn't like \\"streaming\\" quality then buy all means, but this!<br />I'm a sci fi fan and I found these a bit dull. I enjoy the original Star Trek movies as better. General movie goers should stick to Star Wars which are all amazingly done in blu ray.  If you already own those and are looking for something different, I highly recommend these. They are fun movies, just be weary they are not for everybody. Overall I welcome these movies into my collection, I will watch them again except for the third film which I felt was just terrible.

0743454537|5.0|How could she do that? What is he thinking? Why is he not saying anything? How could they do that to their own daughter? How can she do that to her own sister? Is she going to die? These are all the provoking questions and thoughts that came to my mind as I was reading the controversial book My Sisters Keeper. This book will grab your attention and not let you go until the final miracle or last goodbye. Jodi Picoult inserts the reader to make your feel like you are a part of the chaotic family and as your criticize each character you think to yourself well....what would I do?<br /><br />It starts off as a regular family with loving parents and two happy kids growing up. However, when the eldest, Kate, gets diagnosed with leukemia, each member of this once perfect family comes crashing down. Willing to do anything to save Kate, the parents decide to have a perfectly genetically matched daughter in a petri dish so that from her birth Anna will be able to help their daughter live a longer life. As the parents, Brain and Sarah get so caught up with Kate's disease that they cannot see the pain the other two children are going through.<br /><br />Anna, a courageous young women, decides she has had enough when her parents ask her to give up her kidney and that she is finished with the hospital procedures and wants to live a normal life. She can no longer be controlled by the condition of Kate. Before she was born, her parents where already thinking about procedures for her to go through so that she would be able to help Kate. \\"It made me wonder, though, what would have happened if Kate had been healthy. Chances are, I'd still be floating up in heaven or wherever, waiting to be attached to a body to spend some time on Earth.\\"  She collects all the money she can and hires a lawyer to get her medically emancipated from her parents. She has decided that she no longer wants to be the lifeline for Kate anymore. Or at least that what she makes us believe...<br /><br />And then there was Jesse. He on the other hand can no longer handle to lack of attention. He feels he has no purpose in his family because he was not a genetic match and could not save his sister. He turns his way of life to drugs, alcohol, fires and other criminal works. Jodi Picoult's writing style throughout the character of Jesse consists of very dark and dangerous acts. As Jesse reacts to his sister death he states. \\"I imagined the raindrops melting away my skin. I waited for the one stroke of lightning that would arrow through my heart and make me feel one hundred percent alive for the first time in my whole sorry existence.\\" He goes back to a flashback on drugs where he would rather have lightning strike him in the heart than have to go back to the reality of his family and live through his sister death. Even though it would kill him he would still feel more alive and less pain then the pain he feels watching his sister slowly die one earth. She wrote from the perspective of everyone involved in Kate's life so that the reader gets a whole different story from each character and you come to realize how controversial and how difficult this situation is no matter your relationship to someone who is dying. After reading this book, not only did I just want to read it again but I also decided to read another one of her books called Nineteen Minutes. Picoult is an excellent writer who will leave you flabbergasted after every chapter.

B003YL4LYI|5.0|Spoiler alert.  Don't read this if you haven't already ready the 3rd or 4th books.<br /><br />Ok people, those of you who are saying this book is boring or that there is no \\"plot advancement\\" obviously aren't true fantasy/sci-fan fans and/or have no understanding or appreciation of the amount of time and talent that went into the making of a book like this (much less the entire series). How can you say there is no plot advancement when he kills off major (and minor) characters at the most unexpected times, takes plot twists when you least expect it, and ends chapters (and entire books) on cliffhangers that leave you on the edge of your seat wondering what is going to happen next?  Yes, there is \\"downtime\\" for the characters and yes, they have self-reflection and think and ponder on things.  That's what makes them real, believable, and relatable.  This is what's called character development.  And GRRM does character development on a level I have seen few authors accomplish.  GRRM is a born writer, truly talented in a way that others can only dream of.<br /><br />I actually like that he takes you so far in depth into the characters rather than just having random \\"bad guys\\" throughout who are presented as such and you never hear their side of the story, what made them that way. Instead you are just forced into the only point of view that the author gives you which is that this is just a bad person.  But maybe something bad happened to him when he was younger, maybe he doesn't want to be a bad person and wants to redeem himself, or maybe he really is just truly evil.  By giving the backstory to characters with such a high level of development, he lets the readers form their own opinions rather than just telling them what to think.  I mean, I actually cried when Robb, Grey Wind and Catelyn were killed and when Brienne and Podrick were killed.  Maybe some of you will think that is lame, but I think it shows you how compelling these characters are and how relatable they are.  I wouldn't have been nearly as sad about Brienne and Podrick if I hadn't known as much about them as I did.<br /><br />Anyway, I say it's a GREAT book, and a GREAT series.  If you truly LOVE fantasy/sci-fan then you will LOVE these books.  But if you get bored easily, are highly impatient, or just don't CARE about characters the way I do, then don't waste your time reading these books.  Everyone is entitled to their own opinions, but don't knock something and say negative things if you aren't even a true fan of the author or the genre.  True fantasy fans have a fine appreciation for a good, hearty fantasy read, something deep and gritty and powerful, not your fru-fru pop lit damstel-in-distress-princesses-who-wait-for-knights-to-save-them-fall-madly-in-love-and-kill-the-evil-monster books. 0307719219|4.0|3.5 stars: Acemoglu and Robinson's central thesis isn't hard to understand: countries with inclusive, equitable political and economic institutions tend to prosper, while those with extractive, exclusive institutions geared towards the interests of a small elite tend to languish. The authors minimize geography and culture as significant factors in the equation, pointing to nations where those realities are similar but political systems vary.<br /><br />The dynamic exists, the authors maintain, because the interests of an exploitative elite and those of regular citizens are usually in conflict, so the elite must actively block democratic movements, workers rights, unions, property rights, innovation, etc. in order to maintain a hold on power. In more inclusive systems, meanwhile, there is a virtuous circle effect, in which opportunity breeds motivation and meaningful choice, while making it hard for anyone to consolidate too much power over others.<br /><br />It's a strikingly simple hypothesis -- a little too simple, I think -- but the authors back it up with a wide set of historical cases, ranging from post-Renaissance Europe, to the colonial Americas (noting the different approaches taken by English and Spanish settlers in controlling their territories), to post 17th century Britain, to the United States (monopolies and trusts are discussed), to the Arab world, to the Soviet Union, to modern Africa, to North versus South Korea. Even if you more or less accept the book's ideas, the details are still informative. If you're not familiar with the political differences between imperial Spain and England, they cast quite a bit of light on the separate paths taken by the two former world powers -- and their former colonial possessions. Similarly, you don't appreciate what apartheid meant for South Africa until you've contemplated just how the system was structured to impede blacks from becoming more than cheap sources of labor. As was a problem in the US, too.<br /><br />The examinations seemed politically balanced. Communist governments taking a drubbing, and the authors argue that China's rapid growth as orchestrated by Bejiing is unlikely to be sustainable unless the Party relinquishes more of its grip. But Acemoglu and Robinson also pay attention to how capitalist monopolies undermine democratic ideals, as do weak or corrupt central governments that lack the power to enforce laws and protect individual rights.<br /><br />The book has its blind spots, though. I simply don't agree with the authors that geography doesn't matter. Most wealthy countries, it seems to me, have inclusive systems, but were also blessed in resources, either obtained locally, or extracted from some other region. It's easy, for example, to see a country like the Netherlands as owing its prosperity to a liberal democracy that overcame a lack of natural gifts, but that's not the whole story. The Netherlands got started on a path to prosperity because it set up exploitative trading companies during the colonial era and eventually reallocated the wealth into new ventures. Being \\"inclusive\\" at home or among upper classes doesn't mean the same was true abroad or among lower classes. I also think that geopolitics is underrepresented as a factor in Why Nations Fail. South Korea and Israel might be successful countries in spite of tough landscapes, but both enjoyed massive military and economic support from the United States, enabling technological economies to flourish. It's not that a country like Zimbabwe has no chance of becoming a technology center, but it would have to find a way to produce skilled workers who can compete in the global economy, without being tempted to emigrate. Creating an educated population has significant start-up costs, even with the best of intentions.<br /><br />All in all, the ideas that Acemoglu and Robinson promote are important foundational ones, but should be considered with their blind spots taken into account. Readers interestedin history for its own sake might enjoy the case studies; if not, the themes are pretty repetitive.

Death has a weird, dry, and entertaining type of humor, that's great with teen or young adult readers. Zusak introduces the thought-provoking possibility that Death may be just as afraid of us as we are afraid of death. That maybe this fear should not be directed at Death, but at the many circumstances and sequence of events that lead to death. The way the story is narrated is a little hard to understand at first, but worry not, because you'll get accustomed to it, and the story speeds up fairly quickly. This book is also very emotional... left me in a mess of snot and tears. I feel that it has changed the way I see things with its symbolism and artful execution.<br />This book is a must read, and will definitely leave you asking questions that usually do not have solid, black and white answers.

B0070NSPCU|5.0|First let me say that this book was pretty long but it never dragged at all. I never got bored reading this book and found myself tearing through pages eager to get to the next chapter.<br /><br />One of the greatest parts of this book is that in reading the one book I felt like I had completed an entire epic fantasy series. I cant believe that this whole story was contained in one book, I felt like I just spend 40$ on a whole series but in truth I spent 3$ on an amazing book.<br /><br />Plot Review:<br /><br />Vaelin is the son of a renowned war hero. After his mother dies at a young age Vaelins father brings him to the door steps of the \\"6th order\\", which is an order completely devoted to the art of war and killing. The kingdom has 6 orders all devoted to different aspects of the kingdom: War, Healing, Communicating with the dead, scholarly ambitions, etc. All 6 of these orders makes up \\"The Faith\\" which is the kingdoms primary religion. Although all 6 orders concentrate on different areas of service, all are taught and expected to uphold at all times the doctrine of their religion.<br /><br />The book begins as many fantasy series do. Vaelin is cast into a world he is unfamiliar with by circumstances out of his control. He quickly distinguishes himself in this new world where friends can quickly become the deceased and the only enemy is the \\"tests\\" that he has to pass at each stage of his training. Death is a common theme throughout this book and throughout young Vaelins academy.<br /><br />***SPOILER ALERT***<br /><br />Although I will not give away much I will say that throughout this book Vaelin discovers more and more about the faith that he has devoted himself fully to. It ends up not being exactly what he expects and finds that his eyes are being opened more and more to the harsh realities of deceit and manipulation.<br /><br />Eventually after graduating he finds himself in the crosshairs of a crooked king. This king is held as the leader of the faith andis known to as close of a deity as is allowed in their religion. The first chapter reveals Vaelins blind dedication to the faith where the last chapter shows Vaelins eyes finally fully opened to the truths that were held in secret.<br /><br />The king thrusts Vaelin into a world of war and conquest. Through his conquests Vaelin discovers more and more of the web of lies that the king has strung for his own motives. But the even greater revelation to our main character is that the corrupt king is the least of his worries as he finds himself a major player in an even bigger and more ancient enemy.<br /><br />Throughout the whole book Vaelin uncovers a secret power within himself that helps and guides him through the tribulations of his life. It increases in power and is held a secret as he discovers more about his power and more about himself.<br /><br />************************************************<br /><br />This was a great story that held me hooked the whole time. I can not believe it was only 3$ as it was absolutely worth more than that. Some of the great things about this book is that the author is not SCARED! So many authors are scared of how they choose to twist the plot will not be received well by its audience. So the whole story is steered by how something will be taken rather than how the author envisioned it to go. This is absolutely not the case in this book, the author holds no fear; this is his story like it or hate it it will be what he wants it to be. I couldnt have been happier that he did not succumb to the urge to do what was expected instead of being risky.<br /><br />The twists and turns of this book left me shocked and shaken at every turn. To be honest after finishing this story I still have a seed of regret in my heart of how it turned out. Would I have changed it if I had the chance? No way, it makes the story real and unique. Yet still, it does not have the cooky cutter ending that we all in the fantasy world have become accustomed to.<br /><br />If I had to describe this book in 1 word it would be: Complete. The book from start to finish tells such a complete story that it is hard to believe that the title holds the words \\"Book 1\\". The character depth of the main character is impressive. You come to know his devotion is so deep seeded in his character that he would not be who he is without it. Although the character depth in other characters in the book is shallow, I believe that plays perfectly into the theme of the book.<br /><br />The story is meant to get you into Vaelins head. Just as we, as real people, do not always know what is going on in peoples minds around us so in this book you are on the inside looking out. You do not know exactly what is going on in those around you and your opinions of people are based off of the observations and experiences of the main character, not off of some extra point of view that you have that is not shared with the main character. You understand why the character does what he does because you only have the same amount of insight that he has, nothing more nothing less.<br /><br />If I had to say what I found most impressive about this book it would be this: It is very, very, VERY hard to write a book that does not follow a specific chronological set of events. Patrick Rothfuss is one of the only authors I have seen do this style of writing with any sense of clarity. Authors brave enough to attempt to write in this style often leave the reader confused and starting over chapters trying to find out where they got lost. This did not happen to me once in this book. Times are constantly changed throughout the book as Vaelin relives his experiences to a skeptical scribe. He often jumps from the past to the present and many facts are presented in the present that do not start to make sense until you read more and more from the past. In the end the complete picture comes together and you finally understand what was presented in chapter 1. This shows a clear mind ofthe author and a lot of thought and planning. I found that it was my favorite part of the book and it led to an exciting read as more and more things began to make sense the more I continued in the story. I found myself striving to read more so that my ignorance could be cured by Vaelins life experiences.<br /><br />Overall this book was entertaining and well worth the money. For those familiar with fantasy series there are no wizards blowing up towers with a word and a bead of sweat on his brow and there are no fierce dragons that find friendship in a lowly farm hand. The fantasy aspect of this book is much more subtle but no less entertaining. I believe in future books we will see more of the fantasy aspect of this series developed. I recommend this book to anyone, even someone who is not a usual fantasy reader. It is a great read and left me wanting for more. I was excited to find a cliff hanger at the end that will result in a continuation of the story.<br /><br />In closing let me say that I hate when books leave off cliffhangers that are Humungous! You leave feeling unfulfilled and cheated. By the time the next book comes out you have completely forgotten what you were supposed to remember from the last book. This book has a cliffhanger but it only comes after completely developing and deploying the points in this first book. I will be happy to pick up where I left off when the next book comes out.<br /><br />BTW, Anyone have an idea when that will be???<br /><br />I hope this review encourages you to try this book you will not be disappointed.

B000EHSVQ4|3.0|I'm a huge fan of historical dramas, especially when the filmmaker has the vision and budget to really capture the look, feel and spectacle of past events.  When it was announced that Ridley Scott was going to make a film about the Crusades, I was really excited about the possibilities... this director knows how to manage big budgets, big sets and action scenes involving casts of thousands and get it onto the screen. Scott is a superb technical film maker and an intelligent guy.<br />Nonetheless, Kingdom of Heaven suffers too much from the Hollywood treatment and I'm not talking about the casting choices as some reviewers were quick to criticize.<br /><br />Orlando Bloom, as Balian of Ibelin, is just fine in the lead role, as are the majority of others cast in the film. The Muslim actor who portrays Saladin gives a particularly noteworthy and fairly accurate account of this historical figure.  Nope, the fault is with Bill Monahan's script (and Scott sticking to it, so faithfully)  Instead of showing Balian in a reasonably accurate manner as the wealthy inheritor of an estate in the Holy land, he's depicted as a troubled blacksmith- one who's wife has just committed suicide and who's brother is a psychotic and manipulative priest. In a long, convoluted series of scenes, Balian kills his brother, is reconnected with his long lost father who is shortly mortally wounded. He takes up the Cross (reluctantly) and arrives in Messina, ready to travel to the Holy Lands and mange his fathers estate.... but not before he's been shipwrecked !<br /><br />It takes nearly a hour of this convoluted fiction to set the stage for the religious, political and military conflicts that are to come.  Monahan finally settles down and produces some moments of clarity when Balian arrives in the Holy Land.  Of course, any historian of the Crusades could find fault in nearly ever scene, but who in the general audience would notice or care.  We should expect that in a Hollywood extravaganza, a romantic interest would be created, where none actually existed -and that's OK, too!<br /><br />Screen writer Monahan, however, is not content with simply fleshing out and elaborating on the fascinating characters of Baldwin the Leper King, his sister Sibylla, her eventual husband King Guy d' Lusignan and the rogue robber baron Reynald d'Chatillon - he fashions them into over-the-top cartoons.  There's nothing at all subtle or nuanced about the way these characters are depicted. Guy has all the emotional depth of Snidely Whiplash, while Balian plays his adversary as the slightly morose Dudley Do-Right.  Reynald is reduced to a psychopathic killer, rather than a shrewd, albeit violent, political operator. The leper king is gentle, wise and always diplomatic, to the point where it seems he's been sedated throughout Monahan's script.<br /><br />Of course, when it comes to the broader military conflict, Ridley Scott is in his directorial element and the movie captures plenty of thrilling battles, inglorious defeats and individual heroics, while keeping fairly true to the historical record. This is where Kingdom of Heaven borders on being a cinematic classic and there are awesome spectacles of Jerusalem under siege.  Certainly, Ridley Scott and Monahan had the right idea - post 9/11 - to speak to the divisive nature that religion sparks in humanity and to question how theology, greed and power can corrupt peaceful cooperation.  This movie could have been better received and gotten its core message across to a broader audience, had it been more careful to avoid the obvious cliche's of the genre.  I don't even know where to begin discussing in how lousy the script concludes.<br /><br />Hey, the boxed set does contain a nifty full length documentary about the making of Kingdom of Heaven which is really enjoyable for fans of cinema - showing how tens of millions of dollars are spent to bring an epic to the screen, department by department.  That was a fun bonus.  It's a shameall those millions weren't spent crafting a film based on more accurate, and thus meaningful, script.all those millions weren't spent crafting a film based on more accurate, and thus meaningful, script.

B0093M2716|5.0|I first heard of Änglagård through a mail order catalog back in 1996. I acquired an LP copy of Hybris on the Norwegian Colours label the following year (before prices started shooting through the roof). It already went out of print, but I knew I needed to get a copy then or I'd end up forking up the big money for a copy in the future. It blew me away, never realizing prog could be done like that in 1992. Then I acquired Epilog, knowing I needed that one too, and it too blew me away, and no vocals this time. The band broke up in 1994 after their performance at Progfest '94. There was a brief reunion in 2002 minus Tord Lindman, but it was clear by 2003 the reunion didn't last. Now they reunited (again without Tord), and I was sure they'd split after a few shows, but instead they hung around long enough to record a third album, and here it is, Viljans Öga! It was released on their own label, Änglagård Records.<br /><br />Let me tell you right away, if you love their other releases, you need this album! They continue to make amazingly complex and challenging music, with Thomas Johnson providing tons of great Hammond organ and Mellotron, even some Moog. He even uses some Chamberlin sounds too, like some Chamberlin female voices. Bassist Johan Högberg is now going by the name of Johan Brand, and he just piles it with the Rickenbacker bass. Anna Holmgren's flute playing seems to play an even greater role than before. The band seems to work fine without Tord Lindman, leaving all the guitar duty to Jonas Engdegård. All the songs are around the 12-16 minute time range, and there is so much going on. Themes show up, and later on, variations on that same theme, which you might not notice until listening to many times. I love how all the instruments are clearer than anything on their first two albums, Thomas Johnson's Mellotron is more upfront, for example. \\"Ur Vilande\\" starts off with a gentle flute melody, but then soon you get some aggressive passages, and even one passage that sound like didgeridoo-like sound effects (although not from a real didg). \\"Snårdom\\" features some nice Genesis or Camel-like passages with Hackett or Latimer-like guitar passages, plus a slow, somber passage on the Hammond organ. \\"Längtans Klocka\\" has some nice flute themes, and circus music played by guests on wind instruments. It's unbelievable the 18 year gap since their last album, and they stay totally true to themselves, delivering an album just as great as the classics they gave us in the 1990s! It's probably safe to say Viljans Öga is easily the best prog release of 2012, not only that, an album destined to be a classic. I had my copy since early July 2012, and I was listening to it practically nonstop for a month. It blew me away that much. I couldn't believe that prog this good could be made in 2012! Your collection simply isn't complete without it!
1455502758|5.0|In writing this review, I can't help but also feel that I'm writing an obituary.<br /><br />The greatest thing about Hitchens was his humanity. Like the classic figures he so admired he was defined by his flaws as much as by his strengths. Yet, he sure was brilliant. His humour, erudition, pomposity and boldness, combined to form a true tempest of a public speaker. You cannot get bored while listening to Christopher Hitchens.<br /><br />But he is gone now, and for those who miss him, this book is surely his last offering. It could be described as a collection of wandering dispatches from Hitch, as he struggles to acclimatize to life in `tumourtown.' In his `year of living dyingly' the author muses on the inconvenience, humiliation, pain and tedium of his final days with esophageal cancer. He speaks about torture, prayer and predestination, euthanasia, his loss of voice (his very personality fading away). It is a deeply, to be frank, troubling book, given colour and life through Hitch's extraordinary turns of phrase. The New York Times called Elie Wiesel's [[ASIN:0374500010 Night (Oprah's Book Club)]] `a slim volume of terrifying power.' The same description is apt for this work.<br /><br />I miss Christopher Hitchens like almost no other past intellectual hero. I miss him even though on most major respects I disagree with his opinions. I miss him for his humanity, his language, his extraordinary courage. I miss him for that strange twinkle of rebellion that could be seen almost always flickering across his brow.<br /><br />This is, of ninety or so books read so far, my favourite of 2012.

1416550550|4.0|I adored this book. Completely adored it, so much that I may feel compelled to bump the four stars up to five. Morton is a great writer, and while this book had a nice mystery and interwoven clues and all, she could probably write a book with a completely meandering plot and I could still adore it because she writes so beautifully. She can make me sympathetic to a villain and fall love with exactly the character I'm supposed to fall in love with. She can make me curious, passionate, longing, frustrated, and vividly imaginative.<br /><br />So why did I knock off a star? Because she waited about 130 pages to do this. You see, while this book takes place in three different time periods, its true story is the one at the turn of the 20th century. The characters have much more depth, and rightfully so -- the main purpose of the other characters is to uncover the story that took place in the 1900s, and if Morton were to devote the same amount of depth to the characters in each century, the book would be over a thousand pages and the plot would be much less tightly cinched. However, I didn't really get drawn into the story until Eliza Makepeace (as a child) made her first appearance. That didn't happen until somewhere after the hundred page mark, and a really good novel shouldn't take that long to get going.

It only takes minutes per day.  I can do the exercises virtually anywhere and at any time, so even if I am busy, I can squeeze them in.
